↓ <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Bronze_Age" title="Template:Bronze Age"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Bronze_Age" title="Template talk:Bronze Age"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Bronze_Age" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Bronze Age"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Late Bronze Age collapse</b> was a period of <a href="/wiki/Societal_collapse" title="Societal collapse">societal collapse</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_basin" title="Mediterranean basin">Mediterranean basin</a> during the 12th century&#160;BC. It is thought to have affected much of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea#History" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">eastern Libya</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The collapse was sudden, violent, and culturally disruptive for many <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> civilizations, creating a sharp material decline for the region's previously existing powers.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Palace_economy" title="Palace economy">palace economy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_civilization" title="Aegean civilization">Aegean region</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anatolia" title="History of Anatolia">Anatolia</a> that characterized the Late Bronze Age disintegrated, transforming into the small isolated village cultures of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>, which lasted from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1100</span> to <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;750&#160;BC</span>, and were followed by the better-known <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic Age</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> spanning Anatolia and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> collapsed, while states such as the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom of Egypt</a> survived in weakened forms. Other cultures such as the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a> enjoyed increased autonomy and power with the waning military presence of Egypt and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Assyria" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Assyria">Assyria</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a>.
</p><p>Competing theories of the cause of the Late Bronze Age collapse have been proposed since the 19th century, with most involving the violent destruction of cities and towns. These include <a href="/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change" title="Climate variability and change">climate change</a>, volcanic eruptions, droughts, disease, invasions by the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">migrations of the Dorians</a>, economic disruptions due to increased <a href="/wiki/Ironworking" class="mw-redirect" title="Ironworking">ironworking</a>, and changes in <a href="/wiki/Military_technology" title="Military technology">military technology</a> and strategy that brought the decline of <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a> warfare. Following the collapse, gradual changes in <a href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" class="mw-redirect" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">metallurgic technology</a> led to the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> across <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Europe" title="Iron Age Europe">Europe</a>, Asia, and <a href="/wiki/Iron_metallurgy_in_Africa" title="Iron metallurgy in Africa">Africa</a> during the 1st millennium&#160;BC.
</p><p>Scholarship in the late 20th and early 21st century has articulated views of the collapse as being more limited in scale and scope than previously thought.<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:right;" class=""></div>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<span style="font-size:120%">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <span class="nowrap">&#160;&#160;</span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239400231"><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history" title="Template:Human history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history" title="Template talk:Human history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Human_history" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Human history"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The German historian <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Hermann_Ludwig_Heeren" title="Arnold Hermann Ludwig Heeren">Arnold Hermann Ludwig Heeren</a> first dated the Late Bronze Age collapse to 1200&#160;BC. In an 1817 history of Ancient Greece, Heeren stated that the first period of Greek prehistory ended around this time, based on a dating of the fall of <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> to 1190&#160;BC. In 1826, he dated the end of the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Nineteenth Dynasty of Egypt">Nineteenth Dynasty of Egypt</a> to around the same time. Additional events that have been dated to the first half of the 12th century&#160;BC include invasions by the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorians</a>, the fall of Mycenaean Greece and <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">Kassites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, and the carving of the <a href="/wiki/Merneptah_Stele" title="Merneptah Stele">Merneptah Stele</a>—whose inscription includes the earliest attested mention of Israel in the southern Levant<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>—as well as the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">Amorite states</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, the fragmentation of the <a href="/wiki/Luwians" title="Luwians">Luwian states</a> of western Anatolia, and a period of chaos in <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The deterioration of these governments interrupted <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a> and led to severely reduced <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a> rates in much of this area.<sup id="cite_ref-Cultural_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cultural-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Debate">Debate</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Debate"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Initially historians believed that in the first phase of this period, almost every city between <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a> was violently destroyed, and many were abandoned, including <a href="/wiki/Hattusa" title="Hattusa">Hattusa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a>, and Ugarit, with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Drews" title="Robert Drews">Robert Drews</a> claiming that, "Within a period of forty to fifty years at the end of the thirteenth and the beginning of the twelfth century, almost every significant city in the eastern Mediterranean world was destroyed, many of them never to be occupied again."<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>
However more recent research has shown that Drews overestimated the number of cities that were destroyed and referenced destructions that never happened. According to Millek, </p><blockquote><p>If one goes through archaeological literature from the past 150 years, there are 148 sites with 153 destruction events ascribed to the end of the Late Bronze Age ca. 1200 BC. However, of these, 94, or 61%, have either been misdated, assumed based on little evidence, or simply never happened at all. For Drews's map, and his subsequent discussion of some other sites which he believed were destroyed ca. 1200 BC, of the 60 "destructions" 31, or 52%, are false destructions. The complete list of false destructions includes other notable sites such as: Lefkandi, Orchomenos, Athens, Knossos, Alassa, Carchemish, Aleppo, Alalakh, Hama, Qatna, Kadesh, Tell Tweini, Byblos, Tyre, Sidon, Ashdod, Ashkelon, Beth-Shean, Tell Dier Alla, and many more.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Ann Killebrew has shown that cities such as <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> were large and important walled settlements in the pre-Israelite
<a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Middle Bronze</a> IIB and the Israelite Iron Age IIC period (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1800–1550</span> and <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;720–586</span> BC), but that during the intervening <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronze Age collapse">Late Bronze</a> (LB) and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> I and IIA/B Ages sites like Jerusalem were small, relatively insignificant, and unfortified.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some recent writing argues that although some collapses may have happened in this period, these may not have been widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background">Background</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Advanced civilizations with extensive trade networks and complex sociopolitical institutions characterized the Late Bronze Age (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1550</span>–1200 BC).<sup id="cite_ref-:6_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Prominent societies (Egyptians, Hittites, Mesopotamians, and Mycenaeans) exhibited monumental architecture, advanced metallurgy, and literacy.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Flourishing trade in copper, timber, pottery, and agricultural goods,<sup id="cite_ref-:6_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as diplomatic ties progressively deepened their interdependence.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Geopolitical powers of the time relied on variations of the palace economy system, in which wealth is first concentrated in a centralized bureaucracy before being redistributed according to the sovereign's agenda,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a system which primarily benefits the society's elite.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This intricate web of dependencies, coupled with the inflexibility of the palace system, exposed these civilizations to the cascading effects of distant disturbances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014165_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014165-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evidence">Evidence</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Evidence"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Anatolia">Anatolia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Anatolia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hittites_1300bc.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Hittites_1300bc.jpg/220px-Hittites_1300bc.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Hittites_1300bc.jpg/330px-Hittites_1300bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Hittites_1300bc.jpg/440px-Hittites_1300bc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1285" data-file-height="1008" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite Empire</a> around 1300 BC</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many Anatolian sites were destroyed at the Late Bronze Age, and the area appears to have undergone extreme political decentralization. For much of the Late Bronze Age, Anatolia had been dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite Empire</a>, but by 1200 BC, the state was already fragmenting under the strain of famine, plague, and civil war.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Nothing hugely controversial here, leaving this tag to make it clear that the preceding sentences aren&#39;t covered by the Seeher citation. (July 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Hittite capital of <a href="/wiki/Hattusa" title="Hattusa">Hattusa</a> was burned at an unknown date in this general period, though it may in fact have been abandoned at that point.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Karaoğlan,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> near present-day <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>, was burned and the corpses left unburied.<sup id="cite_ref-Drews1995_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drews1995-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many Anatolian sites have destruction layers dating to this general period. Some of them such as <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> were immediately rebuilt, while others such as <a href="/wiki/Kaymak%C3%A7%C4%B1_(archaeological_site)" title="Kaymakçı (archaeological site)">Kaymakçı</a> were abandoned.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>This period appears to have also been a time of migration. For instance, some evidence that the <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a> arrived in Anatolia during this period, possibly through the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a> or over the Caucasus Mountains.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Initially, the Assyrian Empire maintained a presence in the area. However, it gradually withdrew from much of the region for a time in the second half of the 11th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cyprus">Cyprus</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cyprus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>During the reign of the Hittite king <a href="/wiki/Tud%E1%B8%ABaliya_IV" title="Tudḫaliya IV">Tudḫaliya IV</a> (reigned c. 1237–1209&#160;BC), the island was briefly invaded by the Hittites, either to secure the <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> resource or as a way of preventing <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a>. Shortly afterwards, the island was reconquered by his son <a href="/wiki/Suppiluliuma_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Suppiluliuma II">Suppiluliuma II</a> around 1200&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is little evidence of destruction on the island of Cyprus in the years surrounding 1200&#160;BC which marks the separation between the <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Cyprus" title="Prehistoric Cyprus">Late Cypriot II</a> (LCII) from the LCIII period.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The city of <a href="/wiki/Kition" title="Kition">Kition</a> is commonly cited as destroyed at the end of the LC IIC, but the excavator, Vassos Karageorghis, made it expressly clear that it was not destroyed stating, "At Kition, major rebuilding was carried out in both excavated Areas I and II, but there is no evidence of violent destruction; on the contrary, we observe a cultural continuity."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Jesse Millek has demonstrated that while it is possible that the city of Enkomi was destroyed, the archaeological evidence is not clear. Of the two buildings dating to the end of the LC IIC excavated at Enkomi, both had limited evidence of burning and most rooms were without any kind of damage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2021a74–77_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2021a74–77-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The same can be said for the site of Sinda as it is not clear if it was destroyed since only some ash was found but no other evidence that the city was destroyed like fallen walls or burnt rubble.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2021a76_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2021a76-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The only settlement on Cyprus that has clear evidence it was destroyed around 1200&#160;BC was <a href="/wiki/Maa_Palaeokastro" title="Maa Palaeokastro">Maa Palaeokastro</a>, which was likely destroyed by some sort of attack,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2021a79–80_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2021a79–80-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> though the excavators were not sure who attacked it, saying, "We might suggest that [the attackers] were 'pirates', 'adventurers' or remnants of the 'Sea Peoples', but this is simply another way of saying that we do not know."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Several settlements on Cyprus were abandoned at the end of the LC IIC or during the first half of the 12th century BC without destruction such as Pyla <a href="/wiki/Kokkinokremmos" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokkinokremmos">Kokkinokremmos</a>, Toumba tou Skourou, Alassa, and Maroni-Vournes.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a trend which appears to go against much of the Eastern Mediterranean at this time, several areas of Cyprus, Kition and Paphos, appear to have flourished after 1200&#160;BC during the LC IIIA rather than experiencing any sort of downturn.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Greece">Greece</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Greece"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a></div>
<p>Destruction was heaviest at palaces and fortified sites, and none of the Mycenaean palaces of the Late Bronze Age survived (with the possible exception of the <a href="/wiki/Cyclopean" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclopean">Cyclopean</a> fortifications on the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">Acropolis of Athens</a>). Thebes was one of the earliest examples of this, having its palace sacked repeatedly between 1300 and 1200&#160;BC and eventually completely destroyed by fire. The extent of this destruction is highlighted by Robert Drews, who reasons that the destruction was such that Thebes did not resume a significant position in Greece until at least the late 12th century&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrews199322_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrews199322-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many other sites offer less conclusive causes; for example it is unclear what happened at Athens, although it is clear that the settlement saw a significant decline during the Bronze Age Collapse. While there is no evidence of remnants of a destroyed palace or central structure, a change in location of living quarters and burial sites demonstrates a significant recession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrews199325_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrews199325-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Furthermore, the increase in fortification at this site suggests much fear of the decline in Athens. <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Desborough" title="Vincent Desborough">Vincent Desborough</a> asserts that this is evidence of later migrations away from the city in reaction to its initial decline, although a significant population did remain.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It remains possible that this emigration from Athens was not flight from violence. Nancy Demand posits that environmental changes could have played an important role in the collapse of Athens. In particular Demand notes the presence of "enclosed and protected means of access to water sources at Athens" as evidence of persistent droughts in the region that could have resulted in a fragile reliance on imports.<sup id="cite_ref-Demand,_Nancy_H._2011_198_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demand,_Nancy_H._2011_198-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:T%C3%ADryns.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/T%C3%ADryns.jpg/220px-T%C3%ADryns.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/T%C3%ADryns.jpg/330px-T%C3%ADryns.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/T%C3%ADryns.jpg/440px-T%C3%ADryns.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7712" data-file-height="4352" /></a><figcaption>View of the Megaron of the palace at Tiryns, one of the many Greek palaces destroyed during the Bronze Age Collapse</figcaption></figure>
<p>Up to 90% of small sites in the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a> were abandoned, suggesting a major depopulation. Again, as with many of the sites of destruction in Greece, it is unclear how a lot of this destruction came about. The city of Mycenae for example was initially destroyed in an earthquake in 1250&#160;BC as evidenced by the presence of crushed bodies buried in collapsed buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Demand,_Nancy_H._2011_198_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demand,_Nancy_H._2011_198-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the site was rebuilt only to face destruction in 1190&#160;BC as the result of a series of major fires. There is a suggestion by Robert Drews that the fires could have been the result of an attack on the site and its palace; however, <a href="/wiki/Eric_H._Cline" title="Eric H. Cline">Eric Cline</a> points out the lack of archaeological evidence for an attack.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrews199323_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrews199323-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014130_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014130-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus, while fire was definitely the cause of the destruction, it is unclear what or who caused it. A similar situation occurred <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a> in 1200&#160;BC, when an earthquake destroyed much of the city including its palace. It is likely however that the city continued to be inhabited for some time following the earthquake. As a result, there is a general agreement that earthquakes did not permanently destroy Mycenae or Tiryns because, as is highlighted by Guy Middleton, "Physical destruction then cannot fully explain the collapse".<sup id="cite_ref-middleton_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-middleton-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Drews points out that there was continued occupation at these sites, accompanied by attempts to rebuild, demonstrating the continuation of Tiryns as a settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrews199325_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrews199325-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Demand suggests instead that the cause could again be environmental, particularly the lack of homegrown food and the important role of palaces in managing and storing food imports, implying that their destruction only stood to exacerbate the more crucial factor of food shortage.<sup id="cite_ref-Demand,_Nancy_H._2011_198_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demand,_Nancy_H._2011_198-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The importance of trade as a factor is supported by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Spyros_Iakovidis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Spyros Iakovidis (page does not exist)">Spyros Iakovidis</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://el.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%A3%CF%80%CF%8D%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%82_%CE%99%CE%B1%CE%BA%CF%89%CE%B2%CE%AF%CE%B4%CE%B7%CF%82" class="extiw" title="el:Σπύρος Ιακωβίδης">el</a>&#93;</span>, who points out the lack of evidence for violent or sudden decline in Mycenae.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014131_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014131-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Pylos offers some more clues to its destruction, as the intensive and extensive destruction by fire around 1180&#160;BC reflects the violent destruction of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014129_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014129-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is some evidence of Pylos expecting a seaborne attack, with tablets at Pylos discussing "Watchers guarding the coast".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Eric Cline rebuts the idea that this is evidence of an attack by Sea People, pointing out that the tablet does not say what is being watched for or why. Cline does not see naval attacks as playing a role in Pylos's decline.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014129_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014129-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Demand, however, argues that, regardless of what the threat from the sea was, it likely played a role in the decline, at least in hindering trade and perhaps vital food imports.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Bronze Age collapse marked the start of what has been called the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Dark_Ages" title="Greek Dark Ages">Greek Dark Ages</a>, which lasted roughly 400 years and ended with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Archaic Greece</a>. Other cities, such as <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, continued to be occupied, but with a more local sphere of influence, limited evidence of trade and an impoverished culture, from which it took centuries to recover.
</p><p>These sites in Greece show evidence of the collapse:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><a href="/wiki/Iolkos" class="mw-redirect" title="Iolkos">Iolkos</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrews1993_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrews1993-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> – <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> – <a href="/wiki/Kydonia" title="Kydonia">Kydonia</a> – <a href="/wiki/Lefkandi" title="Lefkandi">Lefkandi</a> – <a href="/wiki/Menelaion" title="Menelaion">Menelaion</a> – <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenae</a> – <a href="/wiki/Nichoria" title="Nichoria">Nichoria</a> – <a href="/wiki/Pylos" title="Pylos">Pylos</a> – <a href="/wiki/Teichos_Dymaion" class="mw-redirect" title="Teichos Dymaion">Teichos Dymaion</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://el.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%A4%CE%B5%CE%AF%CF%87%CE%BF%CF%82_%CE%94%CF%85%CE%BC%CE%B1%CE%AF%CF%89%CE%BD" class="extiw" title="el:Τείχος Δυμαίων">el</a>&#93;</span> – <a href="/wiki/Tiryns" title="Tiryns">Tiryns</a> – <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes, Greece</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed"><span title="This being formatted as a block quotation implies it&#39;s a quotation. From what? If it&#39;s not, don&#39;t format it like this (November 2024)">attribution needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Egypt">Egypt</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Egypt"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/End_of_the_19th_Dynasty" title="End of the 19th Dynasty">End of the 19th Dynasty</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg/220px-Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg/330px-Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg/440px-Medinet_Habu_Ramses_III._Tempel_Nordostwand_Abzeichnung_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6352" data-file-height="3149" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> in their ships during the battle with the Egyptians. Relief from the mortuary temple of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_III" title="Ramesses III">Ramesses III</a> at <a href="/wiki/Medinet_Habu_(temple)" class="mw-redirect" title="Medinet Habu (temple)">Medinet Habu</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>While it survived the Bronze Age collapse, the Egyptian Empire of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom era</a> receded considerably in territorial and economic strength during the mid-twelfth century (during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_VI" title="Ramesses VI">Ramesses VI</a>, 1145 to 1137). Previously, the <a href="/wiki/Merneptah_Stele" title="Merneptah Stele">Merneptah Stele</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1200</span>) spoke of attacks (Libyan War) from Putrians (from modern <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>), with associated people of <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(Homer)" title="Achaeans (Homer)">Ekwesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Shekelesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lukka_lands" title="Lukka lands">Lukka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sherden" title="Sherden">Shardana</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Teresh_(sea_people)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Teresh (sea people) (page does not exist)">Teresh</a> (possibly an Egyptian name for the <a href="/wiki/Tyrrhenians" title="Tyrrhenians">Tyrrhenians</a> or <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troas</a>), and a Canaanite revolt, in the cities of <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">Ashkelon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yenoam" title="Yenoam">Yenoam</a> and among the people of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. A second attack (<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Delta" title="Battle of the Delta">Battle of the Delta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Djahy" title="Battle of Djahy">Battle of Djahy</a>) during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_III" title="Ramesses III">Ramesses III</a> (1186–1155) involved <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">Peleset</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tjeker" title="Tjeker">Tjeker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sherden" title="Sherden">Shardana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denyen" title="Denyen">Denyen</a>.
</p><p>The Nubian War, the First Libyan War, the Northern War and the Second Libyan War were all victories for Ramesses. Due to this, however, the economy of Egypt fell into decline and state treasuries were nearly bankrupt. By defeating the Sea Peoples, <a href="/wiki/Libyans" class="mw-redirect" title="Libyans">Libyans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">Nubians</a>, the territory around Egypt was safe during the collapse of the Bronze Age, but military campaigns in Asia depleted the economy. With his victory over the Sea Peoples, Ramesses III stated, "My sword is great and mighty like that of Montu. No land can stand fast before my arms. I am a king rejoicing in slaughter. My reign is calmed in peace." With this claim, Ramesses implied that his reign was safe in the wake of the Bronze Age collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:East-Hem_1000bc.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/East-Hem_1000bc.jpg/220px-East-Hem_1000bc.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="129" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/East-Hem_1000bc.jpg/330px-East-Hem_1000bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/East-Hem_1000bc.jpg/440px-East-Hem_1000bc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3240" data-file-height="1903" /></a><figcaption>The main polities in Eurasia around 1000 BC</figcaption></figure> 
<p>Egypt's withdrawal from the southern Levant was a protracted process lasting some one hundred years and was most likely a product of the political turmoil in Egypt proper. Many Egyptian garrisons or sites with an "Egyptian governor's residence" in the southern Levant were abandoned without destruction including Dier el-Balah, Ashkelon, Tel Mor, Tell el-Far'ah (South), Tel Gerisa, Tell Jemmeh, <a href="/wiki/Tel_Masos" title="Tel Masos">Tel Masos</a>, and Qubur el-Walaydah.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Not all Egyptian sites in the southern Levant were abandoned without destruction. The Egyptian garrison at Aphek was destroyed, likely in an act of warfare at the end of the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Egyptian gate complex uncovered at Jaffa was destroyed at the end of the 12th century between 1134 and 1115 based on C14 dates,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while Beth-Shean was partially though not completely destroyed, possibly by an earthquake, in the mid-12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_Levant">The Levant</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Levant"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Egyptian evidence shows that from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Horemheb" title="Horemheb">Horemheb</a> (ruled either 1319 or 1306 to 1292&#160;BC), wandering <a href="/wiki/Shasu" title="Shasu">Shasu</a> were more problematic than the earlier <a href="/wiki/Apiru" class="mw-redirect" title="Apiru">Apiru</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a> (r. 1279–1213&#160;BC) campaigned against them, pursuing them as far as <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">Moab</a>, where he established a fortress, after a near defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kadesh" title="Battle of Kadesh">Battle of Kadesh</a>. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Merneptah" title="Merneptah">Merneptah</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Shasu" title="Shasu">Shasu</a> threatened the "<a href="/wiki/Via_Maris" title="Via Maris">Way of Horus</a>" north from Gaza. Evidence shows that <a href="/wiki/Deir_Alla" title="Deir Alla">Deir Alla</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sukkot_(place)" title="Sukkot (place)">Succoth</a>) was destroyed, likely by an earthquake, after the reign of Queen <a href="/wiki/Twosret" class="mw-redirect" title="Twosret">Twosret</a> (r. 1191–1189&#160;BC) though the date of this destruction appears to be much later dating to roughly 1150&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is little evidence that any major city or settlement in the southern Levant was destroyed around 1200&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Lachish" class="mw-redirect" title="Lachish">Lachish</a>, the Fosse Temple III was ritually terminated while a house in Area S appears to have burned in a house fire as the most severe evidence of burning was next to two ovens while no other part of the city had evidence of burning. After this though the city was rebuilt in a grander fashion than before.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For Megiddo, most parts of the city did not have any signs of damage and it is only possible that the palace in Area AA might have been destroyed though this is not certain.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the monumental structures at Hazor were indeed destroyed, this destruction was in the mid-13th century long before the end of the Late Bronze Age began.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, many sites were not burned to the ground around 1200&#160;BC including: Ashkelon, Ashdod, Tell es-Safi, Tel Batash, Tel Burna, Tel Dor, Tel Gerisa, Tell Jemmeh, Khirbet Rabud, Tel Zeror, and Tell Abu Hawam among others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2019a147–188_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2019a147–188-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the reign of Ramesses III, <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">Philistines</a> were allowed to resettle the coastal strip from Gaza to Joppa, <a href="/wiki/Denyen" title="Denyen">Denyen</a> (possibly the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Dan" title="Tribe of Dan">tribe of Dan</a> in the Bible, or more likely the people of Adana, also known as Danuna, part of the Hittite Empire) settled from <a href="/wiki/Jaffa" title="Jaffa">Joppa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tjekker" class="mw-redirect" title="Tjekker">Tjekker</a> in Acre. The sites quickly achieved independence, as the <i><a href="/wiki/Tale_of_Wenamun" class="mw-redirect" title="Tale of Wenamun">Tale of Wenamun</a></i> shows.
</p><p>Despite many theories which claim that trade relations broke down after 1200 in the southern Levant, there is ample evidence that trade with other regions continued after the end of the Late Bronze Age in the Southern Levant. Archaeologist Jesse Millek has shown that while the common assumption is that trade in Cypriot and Mycenaean pottery ended around 1200&#160;BC, trade in Cypriot pottery actually largely came to an end at 1300&#160;BC, while for Mycenaean pottery, this trade ended at 1250&#160;BC, and destruction around 1200&#160;BC could not have affected either pattern of international trade since it ended before the end of the Late Bronze Age.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2019a180–212_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2019a180–212-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He has also demonstrated that trade with Egypt continued after 1200&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2019a217–238_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2019a217–238-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Archaeometallurgical studies performed by various teams have also shown that trade in tin, a non-local metal necessary to make bronze, did not stop or decrease after 1200&#160;BC,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> even though the closest sources of the metal were modern Afghanistan, Kazakhstan, or perhaps even Cornwall, England. Lead from Sardinia was still being imported to the southern Levant after 1200&#160;BC during the early Iron Age.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These sites in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Levant" title="Southern Levant">Southern Levant</a> show evidence of the collapse:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><a href="/wiki/Akko" class="mw-redirect" title="Akko">Akko</a> – <a href="/wiki/Ashdod" title="Ashdod">Ashdod</a> – <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">Ashkelon</a> – Beth Shemesh – <a href="/wiki/Bethel" title="Bethel">Bethel</a> – <a href="/wiki/Sukkot_(place)" title="Sukkot (place)">Deir 'Alla (Sukkot)</a> – <a href="/wiki/Tel_Lachish" title="Tel Lachish">Tel Lachish</a> – <a href="/wiki/Tel_Hazor" title="Tel Hazor">Tel Hazor</a> – <a href="/wiki/Tel_Megiddo" title="Tel Megiddo">Tel Megiddo</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed"><span title="Being formatted as a block quotation implies this is a quotation. From what? If it&#39;s not, don&#39;t use this formatting (November 2024)">attribution needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mesopotamia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a> (1392–1056&#160;BC) had destroyed the Hurrian-Mitanni Empire, annexed much of the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Hittite Empire">Hittite Empire</a> and eclipsed the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Empire">Egyptian Empire</a>. At the beginning of the Late Bronze Age collapse, it controlled an empire stretching from the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">Caucasus Mountains</a> in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> in the south, and from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Iran">Ancient Iran</a> in the east to <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> in the west. However, in the 12th century&#160;BC, Assyrian satrapies in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> came under attack from the <a href="/wiki/Mushki" title="Mushki">Mushki</a> (who may have been <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a>) and those in the Levant from Arameans, but <a href="/wiki/Tiglath-Pileser_I" title="Tiglath-Pileser I">Tiglath-Pileser I</a> (reigned 1114–1076&#160;BC) was able to defeat and repel these attacks, conquering the attackers. The Middle Assyrian Empire survived intact throughout much of this period, with Assyria dominating and often ruling Babylonia directly, and controlling southeastern and southwestern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, northwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and much of northern and central <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, as far as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Arameans and <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a> were subjugated, and Assyria and its colonies were not threatened by the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a> who had ravaged Egypt and much of the East Mediterranean, and the Assyrians often conquered as far as <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/East_Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="East Mediterranean">East Mediterranean</a>. However, after the death of <a href="/wiki/Ashur-bel-kala" title="Ashur-bel-kala">Ashur-bel-kala</a> in 1056, Assyria withdrew to areas close to its natural borders, encompassing what is today northern Iraq, northeastern Syria, the fringes of northwestern Iran, and southeastern Turkey. It still retained a stable monarchy, the best army in the world, and an efficient civil administration, enabling it to survive the Bronze Age Collapse intact. Assyrian written records remained numerous and the most consistent in the world during the period, and the Assyrians were still able to mount long range military campaigns in all directions when necessary. From the late 10th century&#160;BC, Assyria once more asserted itself internationally, and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> grew to be the largest the world had yet seen.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The situation in Babylonia was very different. After the Assyrian withdrawal, it was still subject to periodic Assyrian (and <a href="/wiki/Elamite" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamite">Elamite</a>) subjugation, and new groups of Semitic speakers such as the <a href="/wiki/Aramaeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaeans">Arameans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suteans" title="Suteans">Suteans</a> (and in the period after the Bronze Age Collapse, <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a> also) spread unchecked into Babylonia from the Levant, and the power of its weak kings barely extended beyond the city limits of Babylon. Babylon was sacked by the <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elamites</a> under <a href="/wiki/Shutruk-Nahhunte" class="mw-redirect" title="Shutruk-Nahhunte">Shutruk-Nahhunte</a> (c. 1185–1155&#160;BC), and lost control of the <a href="/wiki/Diyala_River" title="Diyala River">Diyala River</a> valley to Assyria.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Syria">Syria</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Syria"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ancient Syria had been initially dominated by a number of indigenous <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a>-speaking peoples. The <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="East Semitic">East Semitic</a>-speaking polities of <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">Ebla</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwest Semitic">Northwest Semitic</a>-speaking Amorites ("Amurru") and the people of Ugarit were prominent among them.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Syria during this time was known as "The land of the Amurru".
</p><p>Before and during the Bronze Age Collapse, Syria became a battleground between the Hittites, the Middle Assyrian Empire, the Mitanni and the New Kingdom of Egypt between the 15th and late 13th centuries BC, with the Assyrians destroying the Hurri-Mitanni empire and annexing much of the Hittite empire. The Egyptian empire had withdrawn from the region after failing to overcome the Hittites and being fearful of the ever-growing Assyrian might, leaving much of the region under Assyrian control until the late 11th century&#160;BC. Later the coastal regions came under attack from the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a>. During this period, from the 12th century&#160;BC, the incoming <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwest Semitic">Northwest Semitic</a>-speaking Arameans came to demographic prominence in Syria, the region outside of the Canaanite-speaking <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> coastal areas eventually came to speak <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> and the region came to be known as <a href="/wiki/Aramea" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramea">Aramea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eber_Nari" class="mw-redirect" title="Eber Nari">Eber Nari</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Babylonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Babylonians">Babylonians</a> belatedly attempted to gain a foothold in the region during their brief revival under <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_I" title="Nebuchadnezzar I">Nebuchadnezzar I</a> in the 12th century&#160;BC, but they too were overcome by their Assyrian neighbors. The modern term "Syria" is a later Indo-European corruption of "Assyria", which only became formally applied to the Levant during the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (323–150&#160;BC) (see <a href="/wiki/Etymology_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="Etymology of Syria">Etymology of Syria</a>).
</p><p>Levantine sites previously showed evidence of trade links with <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akkad_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkad (region)">Akkad</a>, Assyria and <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonia</a>), <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> (Hattia, Hurria, Luwia and later the Hittites), Egypt and the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea#History" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a> in the Late Bronze Age. Evidence at Ugarit shows that the destruction there occurred after the reign of Merneptah (r. 1213–1203&#160;BC) and even the fall of <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_Bay" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellor Bay">Chancellor Bay</a> (d. 1192&#160;BC). The last Bronze Age king of Ugarit, <a href="/wiki/Ammurapi" title="Ammurapi">Ammurapi</a>, was a contemporary of the last-known Hittite king, <a href="/wiki/Suppiluliuma_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Suppiluliuma II">Suppiluliuma II</a>. The exact dates of his reign are unknown.
</p><p>A letter by the king is preserved on one of the clay tablets found baked in the conflagration of the destruction of the city. Ammurapi stresses the seriousness of the crisis faced by many Levantine states due to attacks. In response to a plea for assistance from the king of <a href="/wiki/Alasiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Alasiya">Alasiya</a>, Ammurapi highlights the desperate situation Ugarit faced in letter RS 18.147:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>My father, behold, the enemy's ships came (here); my cities(?) were burned, and they did evil things in my country. Does not my father know that all my troops and chariots(?) are in the Land of Hatti, and all my ships are in the <a href="/wiki/Lukka_lands" title="Lukka lands">Land of Lukka</a>? ... Thus, the country is abandoned to itself. May my father know it: the seven ships of the enemy that came here inflicted much damage upon us.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Eshuwara, the senior governor of Cyprus, responded in letter RS 20.18:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As for the matter concerning those enemies: (it was) the people from your country (and) your own ships (who) did this! And (it was) the people from your country (who) committed these transgression(s) ... I am writing to inform you and protect you. Be aware!<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014151_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014151-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The ruler of <a href="/wiki/Carchemish" title="Carchemish">Carchemish</a> sent troops to assist Ugarit, but Ugarit was sacked. Letter RS 19.011 (KTU 2.61)<sup id="cite_ref-P521115_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P521115-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> sent from Ugarit following the destruction said:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To Ž(?)rdn, my lord, say: thy messenger arrived. The degraded one trembles, and the low one is torn to pieces. Our food in the threshing floors is sacked and the vineyards are also destroyed. Our city is sacked, and may you know it!<sup id="cite_ref-astour_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-astour-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>This quote is frequently interpreted as "the degraded one", referring to the army being humiliated, destroyed, or both.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014151_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014151-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The letter is also quoted with the final statement "Mayst thou know it"/"May you know it" repeated twice for effect in several later sources, while no such repetition appears to occur in the original.
</p><p>The destruction levels of Ugarit contained Late Helladic IIIB ware, but no LH IIIC (see <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a>). Therefore, the date of the destruction is important for the dating of the LH IIIC phase. Since an Egyptian sword bearing the name of <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a> <a href="/wiki/Merneptah" title="Merneptah">Merneptah</a> was found in the destruction levels, 1190&#160;BC was taken as the date for the beginning of the LH IIIC. A <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> tablet found in 1986 shows that Ugarit was destroyed after the death of Merneptah. It is generally agreed that Ugarit had already been destroyed by the eighth year of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_III" title="Ramesses III">Ramesses III</a>, 1178&#160;BC. Letters on clay tablets that were baked in the conflagration caused by the destruction of the city speak of attack from the sea, and a letter from <a href="/wiki/Alashiya" title="Alashiya">Alashiya</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>) speaks of cities already being destroyed by attackers who came by sea.
</p><p>There is clear evidence that Ugarit was destroyed in some kind of assault, though the exact assailant is not known. In one residential area called the Ville sud, thirty two arrowheads were found scattered throughout the area while twelve of the arrowheads were found on the streets and in the open spaces. Along with the arrowheads, two lance heads, four javelin heads, five bronze daggers, one bronze sword, and three bronze pieces of armor were scattered throughout the houses and streets suggesting a fight took place in this residential neighborhood. An additional twenty-five arrowheads were also recovered scattered around the city centre, all of which suggests the city was burnt by an assault not by an earthquake.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the city of Emar, on the Euphrates, at some time between 1187 and 1175 only the monumental and religious structures were targeted for destruction while the houses appear to have been emptied, abandoned and were not destroyed with the monumental structures which suggests that the city was burned by attackers even though no weapons were recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2019b167–169_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2019b167–169-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While certain cities such as Ugarit and Emar were destroyed at the end of the Late Bronze Age, there are several others which were not destroyed even though they erroneously appear on most maps of destruction from the end of the Late Bronze Age. No evidence of destruction has been found at Hama, Qatna, Kadesh, Alalakh, and Aleppo, while for Tell Sukas, archaeologists only found some minor burning on some floors likely indicating that the town was not burned to the ground around 1200&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMillek2019b_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMillek2019b-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic</a> Arameans eventually superseded the earlier Amorites and people of Ugarit. The Arameans, together with the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> and the Syro-Hittite states came to dominate most of the region demographically; however, these people, and the Levant in general, were also conquered and dominated politically and militarily by the Middle Assyrian Empire until Assyria's withdrawal in the late 11th century&#160;BC, although the Assyrians continued to conduct military campaigns in the region. However, with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a> in the late 10th century&#160;BC, the entire region once again fell to Assyria.
</p><p>These sites in Syria show evidence of the collapse:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1244412712"><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><a href="/wiki/Alalakh" title="Alalakh">Alalakh</a> – <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> – <a href="/wiki/Emar" title="Emar">Emar</a> – <a href="/wiki/Hama" title="Hama">Hama</a> – <a href="/wiki/Kadesh_(Syria)" title="Kadesh (Syria)">Kadesh (Syria)</a> – <a href="/wiki/Qatna" title="Qatna">Qatna</a> – <a href="/wiki/Tell_Sukas" title="Tell Sukas">Tell Sukas</a> - <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Attribution_needed" title="Wikipedia:Attribution needed"><span title="Being formatted as a block quotation implies this is a quotation. Of what? If it&#39;s not, don&#39;t use this formatting (November 2024)">attribution needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Possible_causes">Possible causes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Possible causes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Various mutually compatible explanations for the collapse have been proposed, including climatic changes, migratory invasions by groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a>, the spread of iron <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, military developments, and a range of political, social and economic systems failures, but none have achieved consensus. Earthquakes have also been proposed as causal, but recent research suggests that earthquakes were not as influential as previously believed.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is likely that a combination of several factors is responsible.
</p><p>General systems collapse theory, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Tainter" title="Joseph Tainter">Joseph Tainter</a>, proposes that <a href="/wiki/Societal_collapse" title="Societal collapse">societal collapse</a> results from an increase in <a href="/wiki/Social_complexity" title="Social complexity">social complexity</a> beyond a sustainable level, leading people to revert to simpler ways of life.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The growing complexity and specialization of the Late Bronze Age political, economic, and social organization made the organization of civilization too intricate to reestablish once seriously disrupted.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The critical flaws of the Late Bronze Age (its centralization, specialization, complexity, and top-heavy <a href="/wiki/Political_structure" title="Political structure">political structure</a>) were exposed by sociopolitical events (revolt of peasantry and defection of mercenaries), fragility of all kingdoms (Mycenaean, Hittite, Ugaritic, and Egyptian), demographic crises (overpopulation), and wars between states. Other factors that could have placed increasing pressure on the fragile kingdoms include piracy by the Sea Peoples interrupting maritime trade, as well as drought, crop failure, famine, or the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian migration or invasion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECline2014_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECline2014-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Drought">Drought</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Drought"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A diversion of midwinter storms from the Atlantic to north of the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> and the Alps brought wetter conditions to Central Europe and drought to the Eastern Mediterranean near the time of the Late Bronze Age collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-middleton_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-middleton-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During what may have been the driest era of the Late Bronze Age, tree cover of the Mediterranean forest dwindled. Juniper tree ring measurements in the Anatolia region demonstrate a severe dry period from c.1198 to c.1196 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the Dead Sea region (The Southern Levant), the subsurface water level dropped by more than 50 meters during the end of the second millennium BC. According to the geography of that region, for water levels to drop so drastically the amount of rain the surrounding mountains received would have been dismal.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Using the <a href="/wiki/Palmer_Drought_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Palmer Drought Index">Palmer Drought Index</a> for 35 Greek, Turkish and Middle Eastern weather stations, it was shown that a persistent drought like the one that began in January 1972 AD would have affected all of the sites associated with the Late Bronze Age collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, changes at the end of the Bronze Age could be better characterized as a 'gear shift' in Mediterranean climate rather than an event of three years. The long-range shift in precipitation would not have been a crisis event, but rather a continual stress put on societies in the region over several generations. There was no one year where conditions became untenable, "nor one straw that broke the back of the camel".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Analysis of multiple lines of <a href="/wiki/Paleoecology" title="Paleoecology">paleoenvironmental</a> evidence suggests climate change was one aspect associated with this period, but not the sole cause.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was also the conclusion reached by Knapp and Manning in 2016 who concluded that, "Based on a series of <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy</a> indicators, there is clearly some sort of shift to cooler and more arid and unstable conditions generally between the 13th and 10th centuries BC, but not necessarily any one key 'episode'; thus, there is a context for change but not necessarily its only or specific cause."<sup id="cite_ref-:4_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Moreover, Karakaya and Riehl's recent study of ancient plant remains from Syria showed little evidence that plants underwent water stress during the Late Bronze Age to Iron Age transition. As they summarize their research, "The emerging picture as concerns plant subsistence is that there is no clear evidence that the Late Bronze Age and the Iron Age were periods of dearth and widespread famine, as some climate models have presupposed."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ironworking">Ironworking</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ironworking"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Bronze Age collapse may be seen in the context of a technological history that saw the slow spread of <a href="/wiki/Ironworking" class="mw-redirect" title="Ironworking">ironworking</a> technology from present-day <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> in the 13th and the 12th centuries BC.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Leonard R. Palmer suggested that <a href="/wiki/Iron_(material)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron (material)">iron</a>, which is superior to bronze for weapons manufacturing, was in more plentiful supply and so allowed larger armies of iron users to overwhelm the smaller bronze-equipped armies that consisted largely of <a href="/wiki/Maryannu" title="Maryannu">Maryannu</a> <a href="/wiki/Chariotry" class="mw-redirect" title="Chariotry">chariotry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Migratory_invasions">Migratory invasions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Late_Bronze_Age_collapse&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Migratory invasions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dorian_invasion" title="Dorian invasion">Dorian invasion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_Peoples" title="Sea Peoples">Sea Peoples</a></div>
<p>Primary sources report that the era was marked by <a href="/wiki/Pre-modern_human_migration" title="Pre-modern human migration">large-scale migration of people</a> at the end of the Late Bronze Age. Drought in the Nile Valley also may have contributed to the rise of the Sea Peoples and their sudden migration across the eastern Mediterranean. It was suspected that crop failures, famine and the population reduction that resulted from the lackluster flow of the Nile and the migration of the Sea Peoples led to New Kingdom Egypt falling into political instability at the end of the Late Bronze Age and well into the Iron Age.
